<p><b>Wind power</b> is the use of <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">air flow</a> through <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine" title="Wind turbine">wind turbines</a> to <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Mechanical power">mechanically power</a> generators for <a href="/wiki/Electric_power" title="Electric power">electric power</a>. Wind power, as an alternative to burning <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a>, is plentiful, <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable</a>, widely distributed, <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_energy" title="Sustainable energy">clean</a>, produces no <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> emissions during operation, consumes no water, and uses little land.<sup id="cite_ref-landuse_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-landuse-2">[2]</a></sup> The net <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_wind_power" title="Environmental impact of wind power">effects on the environment</a> are far less problematic than those of <a href="/wiki/Nonrenewable_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Nonrenewable power">nonrenewable power</a> sources.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Wind_farm" title="Wind farm">Wind farms</a> consist of many individual wind turbines which are connected to the <a href="/wiki/Electric_power_transmission" title="Electric power transmission">electric power transmission</a> network. Onshore wind is an inexpensive source of electric power, competitive with or in many places cheaper than coal or gas plants.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Offshore wind is steadier and stronger than on land, and <a href="/wiki/Offshore_wind_power" title="Offshore wind power">offshore farms</a> have less visual impact, but construction and maintenance costs are considerably higher. Small onshore wind farms can feed some energy into the grid or provide electric power to isolated off-grid locations.<sup id="cite_ref-Wind_Energy_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wind_Energy-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Wind power gives <a href="/wiki/Variable_renewable_energy" title="Variable renewable energy">variable power</a> which is very consistent from year to year but which has significant variation over shorter time scales. It is therefore used in conjunction with other <a href="/wiki/Electric_power_source" class="mw-redirect" title="Electric power source">electric power sources</a> to give a reliable supply. As the proportion of wind power in a region increases, a need to upgrade the grid, and a lowered ability to supplant conventional production can occur.<sup id="cite_ref-ieawind_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ieawind-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-abbess_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-abbess-8">[8]</a></sup> Power management techniques such as having excess capacity, geographically distributed turbines, <a href="/wiki/Dispatchable_generation" title="Dispatchable generation">dispatchable backing</a> sources, sufficient <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectric_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydroelectric power">hydroelectric power</a>, exporting and importing power to neighboring areas, using <a href="/wiki/Vehicle-to-grid" title="Vehicle-to-grid">vehicle-to-grid</a> strategies or reducing demand when wind production is low, can in many cases overcome these problems.<sup id="cite_ref-eirgrid_impact_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eirgrid_impact-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nicola_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nicola-10">[10]</a></sup> In addition, <a href="/wiki/Weather_forecast" class="mw-redirect" title="Weather forecast">weather forecasting</a> permits the electric power network to be readied for the predictable variations in production that occur.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-huang_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-huang-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of 2015, <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_Denmark" title="Wind power in Denmark">Denmark generates</a> 40% of its electric power from wind,<sup id="cite_ref-euractiv1_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-euractiv1-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-enet42_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enet42-15">[15]</a></sup> and at least 83 other countries around the world are using wind power to supply their electric power grids.<sup id="cite_ref-ren212011_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ren212011-16">[16]</a></sup> In 2014 global wind power capacity expanded 16% to 369,553 MW.<sup id="cite_ref-GWEC-2014_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GWEC-2014-17">[17]</a></sup> Yearly wind energy production is also growing rapidly and has reached around 4% of worldwide electric power usage,<sup id="cite_ref-wwea2014_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wwea2014-18">[18]</a></sup> 11.4% in the EU.<sup id="cite_ref-EWEA2015_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EWEA2015-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_wind_power" title="History of wind power">History of wind power</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wind_turbine_1888_Charles_Brush.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Wind_turbine_1888_Charles_Brush.jpg/220px-Wind_turbine_1888_Charles_Brush.jpg" width="220" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Wind_turbine_1888_Charles_Brush.jpg/330px-Wind_turbine_1888_Charles_Brush.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Wind_turbine_1888_Charles_Brush.jpg/440px-Wind_turbine_1888_Charles_Brush.jpg 2x" data-file-width="714" data-file-height="739" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wind_turbine_1888_Charles_Brush.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Charles_F._Brush" title="Charles F. Brush">Charles Brush's</a> windmill of 1888, used for generating electric power.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Wind power has been used as long as humans have put <a href="/wiki/Sailing_ships" class="mw-redirect" title="Sailing ships">sails</a> into the wind. For more than two millennia <a href="/wiki/Windmill" title="Windmill">wind-powered machines</a> have ground grain and pumped water. Wind power was widely available and not confined to the banks of fast-flowing streams, or later, requiring sources of fuel. Wind-powered pumps drained the <a href="/wiki/Polder#Polders_and_the_Netherlands" title="Polder">polders of the Netherlands</a>, and in arid regions such as the <a href="/wiki/American_mid-west" class="mw-redirect" title="American mid-west">American mid-west</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Australian_outback" class="mw-redirect" title="Australian outback">Australian outback</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wind_pump" class="mw-redirect" title="Wind pump">wind pumps</a> provided water for live stock and steam engines.</p>
<p>The first windmill used for the production of electric power was built in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> in July 1887 by <a href="/wiki/Prof_James_Blyth" class="mw-redirect" title="Prof James Blyth">Prof James Blyth</a> of <a href="/wiki/Anderson%27s_College" class="mw-redirect" title="Anderson's College">Anderson's College</a>, Glasgow (the precursor of <a href="/wiki/Strathclyde_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Strathclyde University">Strathclyde University</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Price_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Price-20">[20]</a></sup> Blyth's 10 m high, cloth-sailed wind turbine was installed in the garden of his holiday cottage at <a href="/wiki/Marykirk" title="Marykirk">Marykirk</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kincardineshire" title="Kincardineshire">Kincardineshire</a> and was used to charge <a href="/wiki/Accumulator_(energy)" title="Accumulator (energy)">accumulators</a> developed by the Frenchman <a href="/wiki/Camille_Alphonse_Faure" title="Camille Alphonse Faure">Camille Alphonse Faure</a>, to power the lighting in the cottage,<sup id="cite_ref-Price_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Price-20">[20]</a></sup> thus making it the first house in the world to have its electric power supplied by wind power.<sup id="cite_ref-Shackleton_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shackleton-21">[21]</a></sup> Blyth offered the surplus electric power to the people of Marykirk for lighting the main street, however, they turned down the offer as they thought electric power was "the work of the devil."<sup id="cite_ref-Price_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Price-20">[20]</a></sup> Although he later built a wind turbine to supply emergency power to the local Lunatic Asylum, Infirmary and Dispensary of <a href="/wiki/Montrose,_Angus" title="Montrose, Angus">Montrose</a> the invention never really caught on as the technology was not considered to be economically viable.<sup id="cite_ref-Price_20-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Price-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>Across the Atlantic, in <a href="/wiki/Cleveland,_Ohio" class="mw-redirect" title="Cleveland, Ohio">Cleveland, Ohio</a> a larger and heavily engineered machine was designed and constructed in the winter of 1887–1888 by <a href="/wiki/Charles_F._Brush" title="Charles F. Brush">Charles F. Brush</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> this was built by his engineering company at his home and operated from 1886 until 1900.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The Brush wind turbine had a rotor 17 m (56 foot) in diameter and was mounted on an 18 m (60 foot) tower. Although large by today's standards, the machine was only rated at 12&#160;kW. The connected dynamo was used either to charge a bank of batteries or to operate up to 100 <a href="/wiki/Incandescent_light_bulb" title="Incandescent light bulb">incandescent light bulbs</a>, three arc lamps, and various motors in Brush's laboratory.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>With the development of electric power, wind power found new applications in lighting buildings remote from centrally-generated power. Throughout the 20th century parallel paths developed small wind stations suitable for farms or residences, and larger utility-scale wind generators that could be connected to electric power grids for remote use of power. Today wind powered generators operate in every size range between tiny stations for battery charging at isolated residences, up to near-gigawatt sized <a href="/wiki/List_of_offshore_wind_farms" title="List of offshore wind farms">offshore wind farms</a> that provide electric power to national electrical networks.</p>

<p>A wind farm is a group of <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine" title="Wind turbine">wind turbines</a> in the same location used for production of electric power. A large wind farm may consist of several hundred individual wind turbines distributed over an extended area, but the land between the turbines may be used for agricultural or other purposes. For example, <a href="/wiki/Gansu_Wind_Farm" title="Gansu Wind Farm">Gansu Wind Farm</a>, the largest wind farm in the world, has several thousand turbines. A wind farm may also be located offshore.</p>
<p>Almost all large wind turbines have the same design&#160;— a horizontal axis wind turbine having an upwind rotor with three blades, attached to a nacelle on top of a tall tubular tower.</p>
<p>In a <a href="/wiki/Wind_farm" title="Wind farm">wind farm</a>, individual turbines are interconnected with a medium voltage (often 34.5 kV), power collection system and communications network. In general, a distance of 7D (7 × Rotor Diameter of the Wind Turbine) is set between each turbine in a fully developed wind farm.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> At a substation, this medium-voltage electric current is increased in voltage with a <a href="/wiki/Transformer" title="Transformer">transformer</a> for connection to the high voltage <a href="/wiki/Electric_power_transmission" title="Electric power transmission">electric power transmission</a> system.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Generator_characteristics_and_stability">Generator characteristics and stability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Generator characteristics and stability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Induction_generator" title="Induction generator">Induction generators</a>, which were often used for wind power projects in the 1980s and 1990s, require <a href="/wiki/Reactive_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Reactive power">reactive power</a> for <a href="/wiki/Excitation_(magnetic)" title="Excitation (magnetic)">excitation</a> so <a href="/wiki/Electrical_substation" title="Electrical substation">substations</a> used in wind-power collection systems include substantial <a href="/wiki/Capacitor" title="Capacitor">capacitor</a> banks for <a href="/wiki/Power_factor_correction" class="mw-redirect" title="Power factor correction">power factor correction</a>. Different types of wind turbine generators behave differently during transmission grid disturbances, so <a href="/wiki/Wind_energy_software" title="Wind energy software">extensive modelling</a> of the dynamic electromechanical characteristics of a new wind farm is required by transmission system operators to ensure predictable stable behaviour during system faults. In particular, induction generators cannot support the system voltage during faults, unlike steam or hydro turbine-driven synchronous generators.</p>
<p>Today these generators aren't used any more in modern turbines. Instead today most turbines use variable speed generators combined with partial- or full-scale power converter between the turbine generator and the collector system, which generally have more desirable properties for grid interconnection and have <a href="/wiki/Low_voltage_ride_through" title="Low voltage ride through">Low voltage ride through</a>-capabilities.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Modern concepts use either <a href="/wiki/Doubly_fed_electric_machine" class="mw-redirect" title="Doubly fed electric machine">doubly fed machines</a> with partial-scale converters or squirrel-cage induction generators or synchronous generators (both permanently and electrically excited) with full scale converters.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>Transmission systems operators will supply a wind farm developer with a <a href="/wiki/Grid_code" title="Grid code">grid code</a> to specify the requirements for interconnection to the transmission grid. This will include <a href="/wiki/Power_factor" title="Power factor">power factor</a>, constancy of <a href="/wiki/Utility_frequency" title="Utility frequency">frequency</a> and dynamic behaviour of the wind farm turbines during a system fault.<sup id="cite_ref-Demeo2005_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Demeo2005-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zavadil2005_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zavadil2005-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Offshore_wind_power">Offshore wind power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Offshore wind power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Agucadoura_WindFloat_Prototype.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Agucadoura_WindFloat_Prototype.jpg/220px-Agucadoura_WindFloat_Prototype.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Agucadoura_WindFloat_Prototype.jpg/330px-Agucadoura_WindFloat_Prototype.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Agucadoura_WindFloat_Prototype.jpg/440px-Agucadoura_WindFloat_Prototype.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3651" data-file-height="2739" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Agucadoura_WindFloat_Prototype.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The world's second full-scale <a href="/wiki/Floating_wind_turbine" title="Floating wind turbine">floating wind turbine</a> (and first to be installed without the use of heavy-lift vessels), WindFloat, operating at rated capacity (2&#160;MW) approximately 5&#160;km offshore of <a href="/wiki/P%C3%B3voa_de_Varzim" title="Póvoa de Varzim">Póvoa de Varzim</a>, Portugal</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Offshore_wind_power" title="Offshore wind power">Offshore wind power</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_offshore_wind_farms" title="List of offshore wind farms">List of offshore wind farms</a></div>
<p>Offshore wind power refers to the construction of wind farms in large bodies of water to generate electric power. These installations can utilize the more frequent and powerful winds that are available in these locations and have less aesthetic impact on the landscape than land based projects. However, the construction and the maintenance costs are considerably higher.<sup id="cite_ref-Hulazan_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hulazan-41">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Millborrow_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Millborrow-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Siemens" title="Siemens">Siemens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vestas" title="Vestas">Vestas</a> are the leading turbine suppliers for offshore wind power. <a href="/wiki/DONG_Energy" title="DONG Energy">DONG Energy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vattenfall" title="Vattenfall">Vattenfall</a> and <a href="/wiki/E.ON" title="E.ON">E.ON</a> are the leading offshore operators.<sup id="cite_ref-btm2010o_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-btm2010o-43">[43]</a></sup> As of October 2010, 3.16 GW of offshore wind power capacity was operational, mainly in Northern Europe. According to <a href="/wiki/BTM_Consult" title="BTM Consult">BTM Consult</a>, more than 16 GW of additional capacity will be installed before the end of 2014 and the UK and Germany will become the two leading markets. Offshore wind power capacity is expected to reach a total of 75 GW worldwide by 2020, with significant contributions from China and the US.<sup id="cite_ref-btm2010o_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-btm2010o-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2012, 1,662 turbines at 55 offshore wind farms in 10 European countries produced 18 TWh, enough to power almost five million households.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> As of August 2013 the <a href="/wiki/London_Array" title="London Array">London Array</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> is the largest offshore wind farm in the world at 630 <a href="/wiki/Megawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Megawatt">MW</a>. This is followed by <a href="/wiki/Gwynt_y_M%C3%B4r" title="Gwynt y Môr">Gwynt y Môr</a> (576 MW), also in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-WalesOnline_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WalesOnline-45">[45]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Collection_and_transmission_network">Collection and transmission network</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Collection and transmission network">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In a <a href="/wiki/Wind_farm" title="Wind farm">wind farm</a>, individual turbines are interconnected with a medium voltage (usually 34.5 kV) power collection system and communications network. At a substation, this medium-voltage electric current is increased in voltage with a transformer for connection to the high voltage <a href="/wiki/Electric_power_transmission" title="Electric power transmission">electric power transmission</a> system.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
<div id="mwe_player_0" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:250px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; style=&quot;width:854px&quot;&gt;&lt;video id=&quot;mwe_player_1&quot; poster=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Vetropark_Ko%C5%A1ava_Zagajica.ogv/854px--Vetropark_Ko%C5%A1ava_Zagajica.ogv.jpg&quot; <div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Vetropark_Ko%C5%A1ava_Zagajica.ogv" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Wind Power in <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A transmission line is required to bring the generated power to (often remote) markets. For an off-shore station this may require a submarine cable. Construction of a new high-voltage line may be too costly for the wind resource alone, but wind sites may take advantage of lines installed for conventionally fueled generation.</p>
<p>One of the biggest current challenges to wind power grid integration in the United States is the necessity of developing new transmission lines to carry power from wind farms, usually in remote lowly populated states in the middle of the country due to availability of wind, to high load locations, usually on the coasts where population density is higher. The current transmission lines in remote locations were not designed for the transport of large amounts of energy.<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-52">[52]</a></sup> As transmission lines become longer the losses associated with power transmission increase, as modes of losses at lower lengths are exacerbated and new modes of losses are no longer negligible as the length is increased, making it harder to transport large loads over large distances.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> However, resistance from state and local governments makes it difficult to construct new transmission lines. Multi state power transmission projects are discouraged by states with cheap electric power rates for fear that exporting their cheap power will lead to increased rates. A 2005 energy law gave the Energy Department authority to approve transmission projects states refused to act on, but after an attempt to use this authority, the Senate declared the department was being overly aggressive in doing so.<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes.com_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-52">[52]</a></sup> Another problem is that wind companies find out after the fact that the transmission capacity of a new farm is below the generation capacity, largely because federal utility rules to encourage renewable energy installation allow feeder lines to meet only minimum standards. These are important issues that need to be solved, as when the transmission capacity does not meet the generation capacity, wind farms are forced to produce below their full potential or stop running all together, in a process known as curtailment. While this leads to potential renewable generation left untapped, it prevents possible grid overload or risk to reliable service.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Wind_power_capacity_and_production">Wind power capacity and production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Wind power capacity and production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_by_country" title="Wind power by country">Wind power by country</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">

<div class="thumbcaption">Global annual new installed wind capacity 1997–2015 (in <a href="/wiki/Megawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Megawatt">MW</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-GWEC-2014_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GWEC-2014-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:3</sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Worldwide there are now over two hundred thousand wind turbines operating, with a total <a href="/wiki/Nameplate_capacity" title="Nameplate capacity">nameplate capacity</a> of 432,000&#160;MW as of end 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Globe_and_Mail_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Globe_and_Mail-56">[56]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> alone passed some 100,000 MW nameplate capacity in September 2012,<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> while the United States surpassed 75,000 MW in 2015 and <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Wind power in the People's Republic of China">China</a>'s grid connected capacity passed 145,000 MW in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Globe_and_Mail_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Globe_and_Mail-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>World wind generation capacity more than quadrupled between 2000 and 2006, doubling about every three years. <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_the_United_States" title="Wind power in the United States">The United States pioneered wind farms</a> and led the world in installed capacity in the 1980s and into the 1990s. In 1997 installed capacity in Germany surpassed the U.S. and led until once again overtaken by the U.S. in 2008. China has been rapidly expanding its wind installations in the late 2000s and passed the U.S. in 2010 to become the world leader. As of 2011, 83 countries around the world were using wind power on a commercial basis.<sup id="cite_ref-ren212011_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ren212011-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>Wind power capacity has expanded rapidly to 336 <a href="/wiki/Gigawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Gigawatt">GW</a> in June 2014, and wind energy production was around 4% of total worldwide electric power usage, and growing rapidly.<sup id="cite_ref-wwea2014_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wwea2014-18">[18]</a></sup> The actual amount of electric power that wind is able to generate is calculated by multiplying the <a href="/wiki/Nameplate_capacity" title="Nameplate capacity">nameplate capacity</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Capacity_factor" title="Capacity factor">capacity factor</a>, which varies according to equipment and location. Estimates of the capacity factors for wind installations are in the range of 35% to 44%.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> By doubling installed capacity an average 40% increase in electricity production would be expected.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Wind power in Europe">Europe</a> accounted for 48% of the world total wind power generation capacity in 2009. In 2010, Spain became Europe's leading producer of wind energy, achieving 42,976 GWh. Germany held the top spot in Europe in terms of installed capacity, with a total of 27,215 MW as of 31 December 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-eolicenergynews4082_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eolicenergynews4082-59">[59]</a></sup> In 2015 wind power constituted 15.6% of all installed power generation capacity in the EU and it generates around 11.4% of its power.<sup id="cite_ref-EWEA2015_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EWEA2015-19">[19]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Growth_trends">Growth trends</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Growth trends">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GlobalWindPowerCumulativeCapacity-withForecast.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/GlobalWindPowerCumulativeCapacity-withForecast.png/220px-GlobalWindPowerCumulativeCapacity-withForecast.png" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/GlobalWindPowerCumulativeCapacity-withForecast.png/330px-GlobalWindPowerCumulativeCapacity-withForecast.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/GlobalWindPowerCumulativeCapacity-withForecast.png/440px-GlobalWindPowerCumulativeCapacity-withForecast.png 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GlobalWindPowerCumulativeCapacity-withForecast.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Worldwide installed wind power capacity forecast<sup id="cite_ref-GWEC_Market_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GWEC_Market-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GWEC_Forcast_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GWEC_Forcast-61">[61]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The wind power industry set new records in 2014– more than 50GW of new capacity was installed. Another record breaking year occurred in 2015, with 22% annual market growth resulting in the 60 GW mark being passed.<sup id="cite_ref-GWEC-Forecast-2016_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GWEC-Forecast-2016-62">[62]</a></sup> In 2015, close to half of all new wind power was added outside of the traditional markets in Europe and North America. This was largely from new construction in China and India. <a href="/wiki/Global_Wind_Energy_Council" title="Global Wind Energy Council">Global Wind Energy Council</a> (GWEC) figures show that 2015 recorded an increase of installed capacity of more than 63 GW, taking the total installed wind energy capacity to 432.9 GW, up from 74 GW in 2006. In terms of economic value, the wind energy sector has become one of the important players in the energy markets, with the total investments reaching <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US$</a>329bn (<a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">€</a>296.6bn), an increase of 4% over 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-GWEC-2015_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GWEC-2015-63">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gwec2007_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gwec2007-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although the <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_industry" title="Wind power industry">wind power industry</a> was affected by the <a href="/wiki/Late-2000s_recession" class="mw-redirect" title="Late-2000s recession">global financial crisis</a> in 2009 and 2010, GWEC predicts that the installed capacity of wind power will be 792.1 GW by the end of 2020<sup id="cite_ref-GWEC-Forecast-2016_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GWEC-Forecast-2016-62">[62]</a></sup> and 4,042 GW by end of 2050.<sup id="cite_ref-GWEC-Forecast-2014_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GWEC-Forecast-2014-65">[65]</a></sup> The increased commissioning of wind power is being accompanied by record low prices for forthcoming renewable electric power. In some cases, wind onshore is already the cheapest electric power generation option and costs are continuing to decline. The contracted prices for wind onshore for the next few years are now as low as 30 USD/MWh.</p>
<p>In the EU in 2015, 44% of all new generating capacity was wind power; while in the same period net fossil fuel power capacity decreased.<sup id="cite_ref-EWEA2015_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EWEA2015-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Capacity_factor">Capacity factor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Capacity factor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since wind speed is not constant, a wind farm's annual <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> production is never as much as the sum of the generator nameplate ratings multiplied by the total hours in a year. The ratio of actual productivity in a year to this theoretical maximum is called the <a href="/wiki/Capacity_factor" title="Capacity factor">capacity factor</a>. Typical capacity factors are 15–50%; values at the upper end of the range are achieved in favourable sites and are due to wind turbine design improvements.<sup id="cite_ref-ceereCapInter_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ceereCapInter-66">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-capacity-factor-50_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-capacity-factor-50-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[nb 1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Online data is available for some locations, and the capacity factor can be calculated from the yearly output.<sup id="cite_ref-MassMaritime_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MassMaritime-69">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-iesoOntarioWind_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iesoOntarioWind-70">[69]</a></sup> For example, the German nationwide average wind power capacity factor over all of 2012 was just under 17.5% (45867&#160;GW·h/yr&#160;/&#160;(29.9&#160;GW&#160;×&#160;24&#160;×&#160;366)&#160;=&#160;0.1746),<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup> and the capacity factor for Scottish wind farms averaged 24% between 2008 and 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-WindPower_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WindPower-72">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>Unlike fueled generating plants, the capacity factor is affected by several parameters, including the variability of the wind at the site and the size of the <a href="/wiki/Electric_generator" title="Electric generator">generator</a> relative to the turbine's swept area. A small generator would be cheaper and achieve a higher capacity factor but would produce less <a href="/wiki/Electric_power" title="Electric power">electric power</a> (and thus less profit) in high winds. Conversely, a large generator would cost more but generate little extra power and, depending on the type, may <a href="/wiki/Stall_(flight)" class="mw-redirect" title="Stall (flight)">stall</a> out at low wind speed. Thus an optimum capacity factor of around 40–50% would be aimed for.<sup id="cite_ref-capacity-factor-50_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-capacity-factor-50-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-capFactors_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-capFactors-73">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2008 study released by the U.S. Department of Energy noted that the capacity factor of new wind installations was increasing as the technology improves, and projected further improvements for future capacity factors.<sup id="cite_ref-Windpowering_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Windpowering-74">[73]</a></sup> In 2010, the department estimated the capacity factor of new wind turbines in 2010 to be 45%.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup> The annual average capacity factor for wind generation in the US has varied between 29.8% and 34.0% during the period 2010–2015.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Penetration">Penetration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Penetration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="wikitable floatright">

<tr>

</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_the_United_States" title="Wind power in the United States">United States</a> (2013)<sup id="cite_ref-usvis_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usvis-83">[82]</a></sup></td>
<td>4.5%</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Wind energy penetration is the fraction of energy produced by wind compared with the total generation. The wind power penetration in world electric power generation in 2015 was 3.5%.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is no generally accepted maximum level of wind penetration. The limit for a particular <a href="/wiki/Electrical_grid" title="Electrical grid">grid</a> will depend on the existing generating plants, pricing mechanisms, capacity for <a href="/wiki/Energy_storage" title="Energy storage">energy storage</a>, demand management and other factors. An interconnected electric power grid will already include <a href="/wiki/Operating_reserve" title="Operating reserve">reserve generating</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electric_power_transmission#Capacity" title="Electric power transmission">transmission capacity</a> to allow for equipment failures. This reserve capacity can also serve to compensate for the varying power generation produced by wind stations. Studies have indicated that 20% of the total annual electrical energy consumption may be incorporated with minimal difficulty.<sup id="cite_ref-tacklingUS_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tacklingUS-86">[85]</a></sup> These studies have been for locations with geographically dispersed wind farms, some degree of <a href="/wiki/Dispatchable_generation" title="Dispatchable generation">dispatchable energy</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hydropower" title="Hydropower">hydropower</a> with storage capacity, demand management, and interconnected to a large grid area enabling the export of electric power when needed. Beyond the 20% level, there are few technical limits, but the economic implications become more significant. Electrical utilities continue to study the effects of large scale penetration of wind generation on system stability and economics.<sup id="cite_ref-NGestimates_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NGestimates-87">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-minnesota_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-minnesota-88">[87]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ESB2004Study_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ESB2004Study-89">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sinclairMerz_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sinclairMerz-90">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>A wind energy penetration figure can be specified for different durations of time, but is often quoted annually. To obtain 100% from wind annually requires substantial long term storage or substantial interconnection to other systems which may already have substantial storage. On a monthly, weekly, daily, or hourly basis—or less—wind might supply as much as or more than 100% of current use, with the rest stored or exported. Seasonal industry might then take advantage of high wind and low usage times such as at night when wind output can exceed normal demand. Such industry might include production of silicon, aluminum,<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup> steel, or of natural gas, and hydrogen, and using future long term storage to facilitate 100% energy from <a href="/wiki/Variable_renewable_energy" title="Variable renewable energy">variable renewable energy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup> Homes can also be programmed to accept extra electric power on demand, for example by remotely turning up water heater thermostats.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Australia, the state of South Australia generates around half of the nation's wind power capacity. By the end of 2011 wind power in South Australia, championed by Premier (and Climate Change Minister) Mike Rann, reached 26% of the State's electric power generation, edging out coal for the first time.<sup id="cite_ref-National_Policy_2012_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-National_Policy_2012-95">[94]</a></sup> At this stage South Australia, with only 7.2% of Australia's population, had 54% of Australia's installed capacity.<sup id="cite_ref-ree2011prelim_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ree2011prelim-79">[78]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-National_Policy_2012_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-National_Policy_2012-95">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Variability">Variability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Variability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Variable_renewable_energy" title="Variable renewable energy">Variable renewable energy</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Grid_balancing" title="Grid balancing">Grid balancing</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Toro_de_osborne.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Toro_de_osborne.jpg/220px-Toro_de_osborne.jpg" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Toro_de_osborne.jpg/330px-Toro_de_osborne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Toro_de_osborne.jpg/440px-Toro_de_osborne.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3872" data-file-height="2538" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Toro_de_osborne.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Wind turbines are typically installed in favorable windy locations. In the image, wind power <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_Spain" title="Wind power in Spain">generators in Spain</a>, near an <a href="/wiki/Osborne_bull" title="Osborne bull">Osborne bull</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Electric power generated from wind power can be highly variable at several different timescales: hourly, daily, or seasonally. Annual variation also exists, but is not as significant. Because instantaneous electrical generation and consumption must remain in balance to maintain grid stability, this variability can present substantial challenges to incorporating large amounts of wind power into a grid system. Intermittency and the non-<a href="/wiki/Intermittent_power_sources#Terminology" class="mw-redirect" title="Intermittent power sources">dispatchable</a> nature of wind energy production can raise costs for regulation, incremental <a href="/wiki/Operating_reserve" title="Operating reserve">operating reserve</a>, and (at high penetration levels) could require an increase in the already existing <a href="/wiki/Energy_demand_management" title="Energy demand management">energy demand management</a>, <a href="/wiki/Load_shedding" class="mw-redirect" title="Load shedding">load shedding</a>, storage solutions or system interconnection with <a href="/wiki/High-voltage_direct_current" title="High-voltage direct current">HVDC</a> cables. The variably of wind is quite different from solar, wind may be producing power at night when other baseload plants are often overproducing.</p>
<p>Fluctuations in load and allowance for failure of large fossil-fuel generating units requires operating reserve capacity, which can be increased to compensate for variability of wind generation.</p>
<p>Wind power is variable, and during low wind periods it must be replaced by other power sources. Transmission networks presently cope with outages of other generation plants and daily changes in electrical demand, but the variability of <a href="/wiki/Intermittent_power_source" class="mw-redirect" title="Intermittent power source">intermittent power sources</a> such as wind power, is more frequent than those of conventional power generation plants which, when scheduled to be operating, may be able to deliver their nameplate capacity around 95% of the time.</p>
<p>Presently, grid systems with large wind penetration require a small increase in the frequency of usage of <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> spinning reserve power plants to prevent a loss of electric power in the event that there is no wind. At low wind power penetration, this is less of an issue.<sup id="cite_ref-is_windpower_reliable_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-is_windpower_reliable-96">[95]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-clavertonReliable_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-clavertonReliable-97">[96]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>GE has installed a prototype wind turbine with onboard battery similar to that of an electric car, equivalent of 1&#160;minute of production. Despite the small capacity, it is enough to guarantee that power output complies with forecast for 15&#160;minutes, as the battery is used to eliminate the difference rather than provide full output. In certain cases the increased predictability can be used to take wind power penetration from 20 to 30 or 40 per cent. The battery cost can be retrieved by selling burst power on demand and reducing backup needs from gas plants.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the UK there were 124 separate occasions from 2008 to 2010 when the nation's wind output fell to less than 2% of installed capacity.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup> A report on Denmark's wind power noted that their wind power network provided less than 1% of average demand on 54 days during the year 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-Denmark_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Denmark-101">[100]</a></sup> Wind power advocates argue that these periods of low wind can be dealt with by simply restarting existing power stations that have been held in readiness, or interlinking with HVDC.<sup id="cite_ref-Czisch-Giebel_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Czisch-Giebel-102">[101]</a></sup> Electrical grids with slow-responding thermal power plants and without ties to networks with hydroelectric generation may have to limit the use of wind power.<sup id="cite_ref-Denmark_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Denmark-101">[100]</a></sup> According to a 2007 Stanford University study published in the <i>Journal of Applied Meteorology and Climatology</i>, interconnecting ten or more wind farms can allow an average of 33% of the total energy produced (i.e. about 8% of total nameplate capacity) to be used as reliable, <a href="/wiki/Baseload_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Baseload power">baseload electric power</a> which can be relied on to handle peak loads, as long as minimum criteria are met for wind speed and turbine height.<sup id="cite_ref-connecting_wind_farms_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-connecting_wind_farms-103">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Archer2007_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Archer2007-104">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>Conversely, on particularly windy days, even with penetration levels of 16%, wind power generation can surpass all other electric power sources in a country. In Spain, in the early hours of 16 April 2012 wind power production reached the highest percentage of electric power production till then, at 60.46% of the total demand.<sup id="cite_ref-eolica_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eolica-105">[104]</a></sup> In Denmark, which had power market penetration of 30% in 2013, over 90&#160;hours, wind power generated 100% of the country's power, peaking at 122% of the country's demand at 2&#160;am on 28 October.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable floatright">
<caption>Increase in system operation costs, Euros per MWh, for 10% &amp; 20% wind share<sup id="cite_ref-ieawind_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ieawind-7">[7]</a></sup></caption>
<tr>
