</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Scout_moor_gearbox,_rotor_shaft_and_brake_assembly.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Scout_moor_gearbox%2C_rotor_shaft_and_brake_assembly.jpg/220px-Scout_moor_gearbox%2C_rotor_shaft_and_brake_assembly.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Scout_moor_gearbox%2C_rotor_shaft_and_brake_assembly.jpg/330px-Scout_moor_gearbox%2C_rotor_shaft_and_brake_assembly.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Scout_moor_gearbox%2C_rotor_shaft_and_brake_assembly.jpg/440px-Scout_moor_gearbox%2C_rotor_shaft_and_brake_assembly.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Scout_moor_gearbox,_rotor_shaft_and_brake_assembly.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Typical components of a wind turbine (gearbox, rotor shaft and brake assembly) being lifted into position</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine" title="Wind turbine">Wind turbines</a> are devices that convert the wind's <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a> into electrical power. The result of over a millennium of <a href="/wiki/Windmill" title="Windmill">windmill</a> development and modern engineering, today's wind turbines are manufactured in a wide range of horizontal axis and <a href="/wiki/Vertical_axis_wind_turbine" title="Vertical axis wind turbine">vertical axis</a> types. The smallest turbines are used for applications such as <a href="/wiki/Battery_charger" title="Battery charger">battery charging</a> for auxiliary power. Slightly larger turbines can be used for making small contributions to a domestic power supply while selling unused power back to the utility supplier via the <a href="/wiki/Electrical_grid" title="Electrical grid">electrical grid</a>. Arrays of large turbines, known as <a href="/wiki/Wind_farm" title="Wind farm">wind farms</a>, have become an increasingly important source of <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a> and are used in many countries as part of a strategy to reduce their reliance on <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a>.</p>
<p>Wind turbine design is the process of defining the form and specifications of a <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine" title="Wind turbine">wind turbine</a> to extract energy from the <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">wind</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BERR_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BERR-243">[241]</a></sup> A wind turbine installation consists of the necessary systems needed to capture the wind's energy, point the turbine into the wind, convert <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_energy" title="Mechanical energy">mechanical rotation</a> into <a href="/wiki/Electrical_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical power">electrical power</a>, and other systems to start, stop, and control the turbine.</p>
<p>In 1919 the German physicist <a href="/wiki/Albert_Betz" title="Albert Betz">Albert Betz</a> showed that for a hypothetical ideal wind-energy extraction machine, the fundamental laws of conservation of mass and energy allowed no more than 16/27 (59.3%) of the kinetic energy of the wind to be captured. This <a href="/wiki/Betz%27_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Betz' law">Betz limit</a> can be approached in modern turbine designs, which may reach 70 to 80% of the theoretical Betz limit.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">[242]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">[243]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine_aerodynamics" class="mw-redirect" title="Wind turbine aerodynamics">aerodynamics of a wind turbine</a> are not straightforward. The air flow at the blades is not the same as the airflow far away from the turbine. The very nature of the way in which energy is extracted from the air also causes air to be deflected by the turbine. In addition the <a href="/wiki/Aerodynamics" title="Aerodynamics">aerodynamics</a> of a wind turbine at the rotor surface exhibit phenomena that are rarely seen in other aerodynamic fields. The shape and dimensions of the blades of the wind turbine are determined by the aerodynamic performance required to efficiently extract energy from the wind, and by the strength required to resist the forces on the blade.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">[244]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to the aerodynamic <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine_design#Blade_design" title="Wind turbine design">design of the blades</a>, the design of a complete wind power system must also address the design of the installation's <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine_design#The_hub" title="Wind turbine design">rotor hub</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nacelle_(wind_turbine)" title="Nacelle (wind turbine)">nacelle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine_design#Tower" title="Wind turbine design">tower structure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electric_generator" title="Electric generator">generator</a>, controls, and foundation.<sup id="cite_ref-univ2011_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-univ2011-247">[245]</a></sup> Further design factors must also be considered when integrating wind turbines into <a href="/wiki/Electrical_grid" title="Electrical grid">electrical power grids</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Wind_energy">Wind energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Wind energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:United_States_Wind_Resources_and_Transmission_Lines_map.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/United_States_Wind_Resources_and_Transmission_Lines_map.jpg/220px-United_States_Wind_Resources_and_Transmission_Lines_map.jpg" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/United_States_Wind_Resources_and_Transmission_Lines_map.jpg/330px-United_States_Wind_Resources_and_Transmission_Lines_map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/United_States_Wind_Resources_and_Transmission_Lines_map.jpg/440px-United_States_Wind_Resources_and_Transmission_Lines_map.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1100" data-file-height="831" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:United_States_Wind_Resources_and_Transmission_Lines_map.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of available wind power for the <a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_the_United_States" title="Wind power in the United States">United States</a>. Color codes indicate wind power density class. (click to see larger)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lee_Ranch_Wind_Speed_Frequency.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Lee_Ranch_Wind_Speed_Frequency.svg/220px-Lee_Ranch_Wind_Speed_Frequency.svg.png" width="220" height="125" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Lee_Ranch_Wind_Speed_Frequency.svg/330px-Lee_Ranch_Wind_Speed_Frequency.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Lee_Ranch_Wind_Speed_Frequency.svg/440px-Lee_Ranch_Wind_Speed_Frequency.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="340" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lee_Ranch_Wind_Speed_Frequency.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Distribution of wind speed (red) and energy (blue) for all of 2002 at the Lee Ranch facility in Colorado. The histogram shows measured data, while the curve is the Rayleigh model distribution for the same average wind speed.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Wind energy is the <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a> of air in motion, also called <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">wind</a>. Total wind energy flowing through an imaginary surface with area <i>A</i> during the time <i>t</i> is:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" >

    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>E</mi>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mfrac>
            <mn>1</mn>
            <mn>2</mn>
          </mfrac>
        </mrow>
        <mi>m</mi>
        <msup>
          <mi>v</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mn>2</mn>
          </mrow>
        </msup>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mfrac>
            <mn>1</mn>
            <mn>2</mn>
          </mfrac>
        </mrow>
        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
        <mi>A</mi>
        <mi>v</mi>
        <mi>t</mi>
        <mi>&#x03C1;<!-- ρ --></mi>
        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
        <msup>
          <mi>v</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mn>2</mn>
          </mrow>
        </msup>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mfrac>
            <mn>1</mn>
            <mn>2</mn>
          </mfrac>
        </mrow>
        <mi>A</mi>
        <mi>t</mi>
        <mi>&#x03C1;<!-- ρ --></mi>
        <msup>
          <mi>v</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mn>3</mn>
          </mrow>
        </msup>
        <mo>,</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle E={\frac {1}{2}}mv^{2}={\frac {1}{2}}(Avt\rho )v^{2}={\frac {1}{2}}At\rho v^{3},}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/768a83c369a7213461cfe88536d02e52d8a1a850" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:37.047ex; height:5.176ex;" alt="E={\frac {1}{2}}mv^{2}={\frac {1}{2}}(Avt\rho )v^{2}={\frac {1}{2}}At\rho v^{3}," /></span><sup id="cite_ref-physics_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-physics-248">[246]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>where <i>ρ</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Density_of_air" title="Density of air">density of air</a>; <i>v</i> is the wind <a href="/wiki/Speed" title="Speed">speed</a>; <i>Avt</i> is the volume of air passing through <i>A</i> (which is considered perpendicular to the direction of the wind); <i>Avtρ</i> is therefore the mass <i>m</i> passing through "A". Note that ½ <i>ρv</i><sup>2</sup> is the kinetic energy of the moving air per unit volume.</p>
<p>Power is energy per unit time, so the wind power incident on <i>A</i> (e.g. equal to the rotor area of a wind turbine) is:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" >
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>P</mi>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mfrac>
            <mi>E</mi>
            <mi>t</mi>
          </mfrac>
        </mrow>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mfrac>
            <mn>1</mn>
            <mn>2</mn>
          </mfrac>
        </mrow>
        <mi>A</mi>
        <mi>&#x03C1;<!-- ρ --></mi>
        <msup>
          <mi>v</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mn>3</mn>
          </mrow>
        </msup>
        <mo>.</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle P={\frac {E}{t}}={\frac {1}{2}}A\rho v^{3}.}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/30739560fad0d0b3435acd7a9acdc0ae045e7306" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:18.428ex; height:5.343ex;" alt="P={\frac {E}{t}}={\frac {1}{2}}A\rho v^{3}." /></span><sup id="cite_ref-physics_248-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-physics-248">[246]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>Wind power in an open air stream is thus <i>proportional</i> to the <i>third power</i> of the wind speed; the available power increases eightfold when the wind speed doubles. Wind turbines for grid electric power therefore need to be especially efficient at greater wind speeds.</p>
<p>Wind is the movement of air across the surface of the Earth, affected by areas of high pressure and of low pressure.<sup id="cite_ref-what_is_wind.3F_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-what_is_wind.3F-249">[247]</a></sup> The global wind kinetic energy averaged approximately 1.50 MJ/m<sup>2</sup> over the period from 1979 to 2010, 1.31 MJ/m<sup>2</sup> in the Northern Hemisphere with 1.70 MJ/m<sup>2</sup> in the Southern Hemisphere. The atmosphere acts as a thermal engine, absorbing heat at higher temperatures, releasing heat at lower temperatures. The process is responsible for production of wind kinetic energy at a rate of 2.46 W/m<sup>2</sup> sustaining thus the circulation of the atmosphere against frictional dissipation.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">[248]</a></sup> A global 1&#160;km<sup>2</sup> map of wind resources is housed at <a rel="nofollow" class="external free" href="http://irena.masdar.ac.ae/?map=103">http://irena.masdar.ac.ae/?map=103</a> , based on calculations by the <a href="/wiki/Technical_University_of_Denmark" title="Technical University of Denmark">Technical University of Denmark</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">[249]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">[250]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">[251]</a></sup> Unlike 'static' wind speed atlases which give a single average speed across multiple years, tools such as <a href="/wiki/Renewables.ninja" title="Renewables.ninja">Renewables.ninja</a> provide time-varying simulations of wind speed and power output from different wind turbine models at hourly resolution.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">[252]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">[253]</a></sup></p>
<p>The total amount of economically extractable power available from the wind is considerably more than present human power use from all sources.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">[254]</a></sup> Axel Kleidon of the <a href="/wiki/Max_Planck_Society" title="Max Planck Society">Max Planck Institute</a> in Germany, carried out a "top down" calculation on how much wind energy there is, starting with the incoming solar radiation that drives the winds by creating temperature differences in the atmosphere. He concluded that somewhere between 18 TW and 68 TW could be extracted.<sup id="cite_ref-nsc2012_257-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nsc2012-257">[255]</a></sup></p>
<p>Cristina Archer and <a href="/wiki/Mark_Z._Jacobson" title="Mark Z. Jacobson">Mark Z. Jacobson</a> presented a "bottom-up" estimate, which unlike Kleidon's are based on actual measurements of wind speeds, and found that there is 1700 TW of wind power at an altitude of 100&#160;metres over land and sea. Of this, "between 72 and 170 TW could be extracted in a practical and cost-competitive manner".<sup id="cite_ref-nsc2012_257-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nsc2012-257">[255]</a></sup> They later estimated 80 TW.<sup id="cite_ref-Jacobson2012_258-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobson2012-258">[256]</a></sup> However research at <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a> estimates 1 Watt/m<sup>2</sup> on average and 2–10 MW/km<sup>2</sup> capacity for large scale wind farms, suggesting that these estimates of total global wind resources are too high by a factor of about 4.<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">[257]</a></sup></p>
<p>The strength of wind varies, and an average value for a given location does not alone indicate the amount of energy a wind turbine could produce there.</p>
<p>To assess prospective wind power sites a probability distribution function is often fit to the observed wind speed data.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">[258]</a></sup> Different locations will have different wind speed distributions. The <a href="/wiki/Weibull_distribution" title="Weibull distribution">Weibull</a> model closely mirrors the actual distribution of hourly/ten-minute wind speeds at many locations. The Weibull factor is often close to 2 and therefore a <a href="/wiki/Rayleigh_distribution" title="Rayleigh distribution">Rayleigh distribution</a> can be used as a less accurate, but simpler model.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">[259]</a></sup></p>

<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 208.66666666667px">

</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/REpower" class="mw-redirect" title="REpower">REpower</a> 5 MW wind turbine under construction at Nigg fabrication yard on the <a href="/wiki/Cromarty_Firth" title="Cromarty Firth">Cromarty Firth</a></p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 255.33333333333px">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wind_power&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-68"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-68">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For example, a 1&#160;MW turbine with a capacity factor of 35% will not produce 8,760&#160;MW·h in a year (1 × 24 × 365), but only 1 × 0.35 × 24 × 365&#160;=&#160;3,066&#160;MW·h, averaging to 0.35&#160;MW.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-110"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-110">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Wind_power_in_California" title="Wind power in California">California</a> is an exception.</span></li>
</ol>

<li id="cite_note-Diesendorf-114"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Diesendorf_114-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Diesendorf, Mark (2007). "<a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_Solutions_with_Sustainable_Energy" title="Greenhouse Solutions with Sustainable Energy">Greenhouse Solutions with Sustainable Energy</a>": 119. <q>Graham Sinden analysed over 30 years of hourly wind speed data from 66 sites spread out over the United Kingdom. He found that the correlation coefficient of wind power fell from 0.6 at 200&#160;km to 0.25 at 600&#160;km separation (a perfect correlation would have a coefficient equal to 1.0.) There were no hours in the data set where wind speed was below the cut-in wind speed of a modern wind turbine throughout the United Kingdom, and low wind speed events affecting more than 90 per cent of the United Kingdom had an average recurrent rate of only one hour per year.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AWind+power&amp;rft.atitle=Greenhouse+Solutions+with+Sustainable+Energy&amp;rft.au=Diesendorf%2C+Mark&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.pages=119&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
